<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Trait that determines an individual's sexually reproductive function</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about sex in sexually reproducing organisms. For the act, see <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">Sexual intercourse</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sex_(disambiguation)" title="Sex (disambiguation)">Sex (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sex_sidebar" title="Template:Sex sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sex_sidebar" title="Template talk:Sex sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sex_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sex</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_trait" title="Phenotypic trait">trait</a> that determines whether a <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexually reproducing</a> animal or plant produces <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gametes" title="Gametes">gametes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a> ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevenson_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purves_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purves-2">[2]</a></sup> Male plants and animals produce smaller gametes (spermatozoa, <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>) while females produce larger ones (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a>, often called egg cells).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-3">[3]</a></sup> Organisms that produce both types of gametes are called <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purves_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Purves-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup>  During sexual reproduction, male and female gametes fuse to form <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygotes</a>, which develop into <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a> that inherit traits from each parent.
</p><p>Males and females of a species may have physical similarities (sexual monomorphism) or differences (<a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>) that  reflect various reproductive pressures on the respective sexes. <a href="/wiki/Mate_choice" title="Mate choice">Mate choice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> can accelerate the evolution of physical differences between the sexes.
</p><p>The terms <i>male</i> and <i>female</i> typically do not apply in sexually undifferentiated species in which the individuals are <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#isomorphic" title="Glossary of botanical terms">isomorphic</a> (look the same) and the gametes are <a href="/wiki/Isogamy" title="Isogamy">isogamous</a> (indistinguishable in size and shape), such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorophyte" title="Chlorophyte">green alga</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ulva_lactuca" title="Ulva lactuca">Ulva lactuca</a></i>. Some kinds of functional differences between gametes, such as in <a href="/wiki/Mating_in_fungi" title="Mating in fungi">fungi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mooretal_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mooretal-5">[5]</a></sup> may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Mating_type" title="Mating type">mating types</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumar-20192_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar-20192-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>A living thing's sex is determined by its genes.  Most <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> have the <a href="/wiki/XY_sex-determination_system" title="XY sex-determination system">XY sex-determination system</a>, where male <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> carry an X and a <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a> (XY), whereas female mammals carry two <a href="/wiki/X_chromosome" title="X chromosome">X chromosomes</a> (XX). Other chromosomal <a href="/wiki/Sex-determination_system" title="Sex-determination system">sex-determination systems</a> in animals include the <a href="/wiki/ZW_sex-determination_system" title="ZW sex-determination system">ZW system</a> in birds, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X0_sex-determination_system" title="X0 sex-determination system">X0 system</a> in insects. Various <a href="/wiki/Environmental_sex_determination" title="Environmental sex determination">environmental systems</a> include <a href="/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination">temperature-dependent sex determination</a> in reptiles and crustaceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_reproduction">Sexual reproduction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Isogamy" title="Isogamy">Isogamy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anisogamy" title="Anisogamy">Anisogamy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sexual_cycle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sexual_cycle.svg/220px-Sexual_cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sexual_cycle.svg/330px-Sexual_cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sexual_cycle.svg/440px-Sexual_cycle.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sexual_cycle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The life cycle of sexually reproducing organisms cycles through haploid and diploid stages</div></div></div>
<p>Sexual reproduction is a process exclusive to <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> in which organisms produce offspring that possess a selection of the genetic traits of each parent. Genetic traits are contained within the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA) of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>. The cells of eukaryotes have a set of paired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homologous_chromosomes" title="Homologous chromosomes">homologous chromosomes</a>, one from each parent, and this double-chromosome  stage is called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a>". During sexual reproduction, diploid organisms produce specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> sex cells called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gametes" title="Gametes">gametes</a> via <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> each of which has a single set of chromosomes. Meiosis involves a stage of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">genetic recombination</a>  via <a href="/wiki/Chromosomal_crossover" title="Chromosomal crossover">chromosomal crossover</a>, in which regions of DNA are exchanged between matched pairs of chromosomes, to form new chromosomes each with a new and unique combination of the genes of the parents. Then the chromosomes are separated into single sets in the gametes. Each gamete in the offspring thus has half of the genetic material of the mother and half of the father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The combination of chromosomal crossover and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a>, bringing the two single sets of chromosomes together to make a new diploid <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>, results in new organisms that contain different sets of the genetic traits of each parent.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, the haploid stage only occurs in the gametes, the haploid cells that are specialized to fuse to form a zygote that develops into a new diploid organism. In <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, the diploid organism produces haploid <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> by meiosis that are capable of undergoing repeated <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">cell division</a> to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> haploid organisms. In either case, gametes may be externally similar (<a href="/wiki/Isogamy" title="Isogamy">isogamy</a>) as in the green alga <i><a href="/wiki/Ulva" title="Ulva">Ulva</a></i> or may be different in size and other aspects (<a href="/wiki/Anisogamy" title="Anisogamy">anisogamy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>  The size difference is greatest in <a href="/wiki/Oogamy" title="Oogamy">oogamy</a>, a type of anisogamy in which a small, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motile" title="Motile">motile</a> gamete combines with a much larger, non-motile gamete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>By convention, the larger gamete (called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ovum</a>, or egg cell) is considered female, while the smaller gamete (called a spermatozoon, or sperm cell) is considered male. An individual that produces exclusively large gametes is female, and one that produces exclusively small gametes is male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> An individual that produces both types of gametes is a <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a>. In some cases hermaphrodites are able to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-fertilization" title="Self-fertilization">self-fertilize</a> and produce offspring on their own, without the need of a partner.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction#Animals" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction § Animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg/220px-Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg/330px-Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg/440px-Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoverflies_mating_midair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoverfly" title="Hoverfly">Hoverflies</a> mating</div></div></div>
<p>Most sexually reproducing animals spend their lives as diploid, with the haploid stage reduced to single-cell gametes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The gametes of animals have male and female forms—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatozoa" title="Spermatozoa">spermatozoa</a> and egg cells. These gametes combine to form embryos which develop into new organisms.
</p><p>The male gamete, a <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoon</a> (produced in vertebrates within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testes" title="Testes">testes</a>), is a small cell containing a single long <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagellum</a> which propels it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
Spermatozoa are extremely reduced cells, lacking many cellular components that would be necessary for embryonic development. They are specialized for motility, seeking out an egg cell and fusing with it in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization#Fertilisation_in_animals" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a>.
</p><p>Female gametes are egg cells. In vertebrates they are produced within the <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a>. They are large immobile cells that contain the nutrients and cellular components necessary for a developing embryo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Egg cells are often associated with other cells which support the development of the embryo, forming an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">egg</a>. In mammals, the fertilized embryo instead develops within the female, receiving nutrition directly from its mother.
</p><p>Animals are usually mobile and seek out a partner of the opposite sex for <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>. Animals which live in the water can mate using <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>, where the eggs and sperm are released into and combine within the surrounding water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Most animals that live outside of water, however, use <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">internal fertilization</a>, transferring sperm directly into the female to prevent the gametes from drying up.
</p><p>In most birds, both excretion and reproduction is done through a single posterior opening, called the <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloaca</a>—male and female birds touch cloaca to transfer sperm, a process called "cloacal kissing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In many other terrestrial animals, males use specialized <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ" title="Sex organ">sex organs</a> to assist the transport of sperm—these male sex organs are called <a href="/wiki/Intromittent_organ" title="Intromittent organ">intromittent organs</a>. In humans and other mammals this male organ is the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a>, which enters the female reproductive tract (called the <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vagina</a>) to achieve <a href="/wiki/Insemination" title="Insemination">insemination</a>—a process called <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a>. The penis contains a tube through which <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">semen</a> (a fluid containing sperm) travels. In female mammals the vagina connects with the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>, an organ which directly supports the development of a fertilized embryo within (a process called <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a>).
</p><p>Because of their motility, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_sexual_behavior" title="Animal sexual behavior">animal sexual behavior</a> can involve coercive sex. <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_insemination" title="Traumatic insemination">Traumatic insemination</a>, for example, is used by some insect species to inseminate females through a wound in the abdominal cavity—a process detrimental to the female's health.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mature_flower_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="693" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/300px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/450px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/600px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mature_flower_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flowers contain the sexual organs of flowering plants, usually containing both male and female parts.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_reproduction" title="Plant reproduction">Plant reproduction</a></div>
<p>Like animals, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_plants" title="Land plants">land plants</a> have specialized male and female gametes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-19">[19]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Spermatophyte" title="Spermatophyte">seed plants</a>, male gametes are produced by reduced male <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gametophytes" title="Gametophytes">gametophytes</a> that are contained within hard coats, forming <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>. The female gametes of seed plants are contained within <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovules</a>. Once fertilized these form <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> which, like eggs, contain the nutrients necessary for the initial development of the embryonic plant.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright" style="background:#f9f9f9; border:1px solid #ccc; margin:0.5em;">
<table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0" style="width:260px; font-size:85%; border:1px solid #ccc; margin:0.3em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg"><img alt="Pinus nigra cone.jpg" data-file-height="726" data-file-width="612" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg/152px-Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg/228px-Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg/303px-Pinus_nigra_cone.jpg 2x" width="152"/></a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pine_cones,_immature_male.jpg"><img alt="Pine cones, immature male.jpg" data-file-height="1144" data-file-width="661" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg/104px-Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg/156px-Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg/208px-Pine_cones%2C_immature_male.jpg 2x" width="104"/></a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="border: none; width:260px;">Female (left) and male (right) cones contain the sex organs of pines and other conifers.</div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a> contain their sexual organs. Flowers are usually hermaphroditic, containing both male and female sexual organs. The female parts, in the center of a flower, are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pistils" title="Pistils">pistils</a>, each unit consisting of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpel</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Style_(botany)" title="Style (botany)">style</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">stigma</a>. Two or more of these reproductive units may be merged to form a single compound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pistil" title="Pistil">pistil</a>, the fused carpels forming an <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a>. Within the carpels are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovules" title="Ovules">ovules</a> which develop into seeds after fertilization. The male parts of the flower are the <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamens</a>: these consist of long filaments arranged between the pistil and the petals that produce pollen in <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#anther" title="Glossary of botanical terms">anthers</a> at their tips. When a pollen grain lands upon the stigma on top of a carpel's style, it germinates to produce a <a href="/wiki/Pollen_tube" title="Pollen tube">pollen tube</a> that grows down through the tissues of the style into the carpel, where it delivers male gamete nuclei to fertilize an ovule that eventually develops into a seed.
</p><p>Some hermaphroditic plants are self-fertile, but plants have evolved multiple different <a href="/wiki/Self-incompatibility" title="Self-incompatibility">self-incompatibility</a> mechanisms to avoid self-fertilization, involving <a href="/wiki/Sequential_hermaphroditism" title="Sequential hermaphroditism">sequential hermaphroditism</a>, molecular recognition systems and morphological mechanisms such as <a href="/wiki/Heterostyly" title="Heterostyly">heterostyly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Judd_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Judd-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 73, 74">: 73, 74 </span></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pines</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a> the sex organs are produced within <a href="/wiki/Conifer_cone" title="Conifer cone">cones</a> that have male and female forms. Male cones are smaller than female ones and produce pollen which is transported by wind to land in female cones. The larger and longer-lived female cones are typically more durable, and contain ovules within them that develop into seeds after fertilization.
</p><p>Because <a href="/wiki/Spermatophyte" title="Spermatophyte">seed plants</a> are immobile, they depend upon passive methods for transporting pollen grains to other plants. Many, including conifers and grasses, produce lightweight pollen which is carried by wind to neighboring plants. Some flowering plants have heavier, sticky pollen that is specialized for transportation by insects or larger animals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humming_birds" title="Humming birds">humming birds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bats" title="Bats">bats</a>, which may be attracted to flowers containing rewards of nectar and pollen. These animals transport the pollen as they move to other flowers, which also contain female reproductive organs, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mating_in_fungi" title="Mating in fungi">Mating in fungi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiitake_mushroom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1816" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shiitake_mushroom.jpg/220px-Shiitake_mushroom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shiitake_mushroom.jpg/330px-Shiitake_mushroom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shiitake_mushroom.jpg/440px-Shiitake_mushroom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shiitake_mushroom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mushrooms are produced as part of fungal sexual reproduction</div></div></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> reproduce sexually and have both haploid and diploid stages in their life cycles. These fungi are typically <a href="/wiki/Isogamy" title="Isogamy">isogamous</a>, lacking male and female specialization: haploid fungi grow into contact with each other and then fuse their cells. In some of these cases, the fusion is asymmetric, and the cell which donates only a nucleus (and not accompanying cellular material) could arguably be considered "male".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Fungi may also have more complex allelic mating systems, with other sexes not accurately described as male, female, or hermaphroditic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watkinson_Boddy_Money_2015_p._115_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watkinson_Boddy_Money_2015_p._115-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some fungi, including <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">baker's yeast</a>, have <a href="/wiki/Mating_type" title="Mating type">mating types</a> that create a duality similar to male and female roles. Yeast with the same mating type will not fuse with each other to form diploid cells, only with yeast carrying the other mating type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many species of <a href="/wiki/Dikarya" title="Dikarya">higher fungi</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a> as part of their <a href="/wiki/Fungus#Reproduction" title="Fungus">sexual reproduction</a>. Within the mushroom diploid cells are formed, later dividing into haploid <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>. The height of the mushroom aids the <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">dispersal</a> of these sexually produced offspring.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_systems">Sexual systems</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_system" title="Sexual system">Sexual system</a></div><p>A <a href="/wiki/Sexual_system" title="Sexual system">sexual system</a> is a distribution of male and female functions across organisms in a species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-:17-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals_2">Animals</span></h3>
<p>Approximately 95% of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> species have separate male and female individuals, and are said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gonochoric" title="Gonochoric">gonochoric</a>. About 5% of animal species are hermaphroditic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-:17-24">[24]</a></sup> This low percentage is due to the very large number of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a> species, in which hermaphroditism is absent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup> About 99% of <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> are gonochoric, and the remaining 1% that are hermaphroditic are almost all fishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants_2">Plants</span></h3>
<p>The majority of plants are <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#bisexual" title="Glossary of botanical terms">bisexual</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 212">: 212 </span></sup> either hermaphrodite (with both stamens and pistil in the same flower) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoecious_(botany)" title="Monoecious (botany)">monoecious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beentje_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beentje-29">[29]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Dioecy" title="Dioecy">dioecious</a> species male and female sexes are on separate plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> About 5% of flowering plants are dioecious, resulting from as many as 5000 independent origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renner_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renner-31">[31]</a></sup> Dioecy is common in <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a>, in which about 65% of species are dioecious, but most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifers" title="Conifers">conifers</a> are monoecious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walas_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walas-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_sex">Evolution of sex</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_sexual_reproduction" title="Evolution of sexual reproduction">Evolution of sexual reproduction</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gamete#Evolution" title="Gamete">Gamete § Evolution</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:234px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anisogamy.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="262" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Anisogamy.svg/100px-Anisogamy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Anisogamy.svg/150px-Anisogamy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Anisogamy.svg/200px-Anisogamy.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Different forms of <a href="/wiki/Anisogamy" title="Anisogamy">anisogamy</a>:<br/>A) anisogamy of motile cells, B) oogamy (egg cell and sperm cell), C) anisogamy of non-motile cells (egg cell and spermatia).</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:106px;max-width:106px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:234px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isogamy.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="267" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Isogamy.svg/104px-Isogamy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Isogamy.svg/156px-Isogamy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Isogamy.svg/208px-Isogamy.svg.png 2x" width="104"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Different forms of isogamy:<br/>A) isogamy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motile_cell" title="Motile cell">motile cells</a>, B) isogamy of non-motile cells, C) conjugation.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>It is generally accepted that <a href="/wiki/Isogamy#Evolution" title="Isogamy">isogamy was ancestral</a> to <a href="/wiki/Anisogamy" title="Anisogamy">anisogamy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-33">[33]</a></sup> and that anisogamy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolved_independently" title="Evolved independently">evolved several times independently</a> in different groups of eukaryotes, including protists, algae, plants, and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anisogamy#Evolution" title="Anisogamy">evolution of anisogamy</a> is synonymous with the <a href="/wiki/Male#Evolution" title="Male">origin of male</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Female#Evolution" title="Female">origin of female</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:11-34">[34]</a></sup> It is also the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism#Evolution" title="Sexual dimorphism">first step towards sexual dimorphism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-35">[35]</a></sup> and influenced the evolution of various sex differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:102_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-:102-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the evolution of anisogamy has left no fossil evidence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-37">[37]</a></sup> and until 2006 there was no genetic evidence for the <a href="/wiki/Mating_type#Evolution" title="Mating type">evolutionary link between sexes and mating types</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:92_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-:92-38">[38]</a></sup> It is unclear whether anisogamy first led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermaphroditism#Evolution" title="Hermaphroditism">the evolution of hermaphroditism</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Gonochorism#Evolution" title="Gonochorism">evolution of gonochorism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:03-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 213">: 213 </span></sup>
</p><p>But a 1.2 billion year old fossil from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangiomorpha_pubescens" title="Bangiomorpha pubescens">Bangiomorpha pubescens</a></i> has provided the oldest fossil record for the differentiation of male and female reproductive types and shown that sexes evolved early in eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:172_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:172-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original form of sex was <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>. <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">Internal fertilization</a>, or sex as we know it, evolved later<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and became dominant for vertebrates after their <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_land_invasion" title="Vertebrate land invasion">emergence on land</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex-determination_systems">Sex-determination systems</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sex-determination_system" title="Sex-determination system">Sex-determination system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolsex-dia2a.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Evolsex-dia2a.svg/300px-Evolsex-dia2a.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Evolsex-dia2a.svg/450px-Evolsex-dia2a.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Evolsex-dia2a.svg/600px-Evolsex-dia2a.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolsex-dia2a.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sex helps the spread of advantageous traits through recombination. The diagrams compare the evolution of allele frequency in a sexual population (top) and an asexual population (bottom). The vertical axis shows frequency and the horizontal axis shows time. The alleles a/A and b/B occur at random. The advantageous alleles A and B, arising independently, can be rapidly combined by sexual reproduction into the most advantageous combination AB. Asexual reproduction takes longer to achieve this combination because it can only produce AB if A arises in an individual which already has B or vice versa.</div></div></div>
<p>The biological cause of an organism developing into one sex or the other is called <i>sex determination</i>.  The cause may be genetic, environmental, <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploidy" title="Haplodiploidy">haplodiploidy</a>, or multiple factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup>  Within animals and other organisms that have genetic sex-determination systems, the determining factor may be the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Sex_chromosome" title="Sex chromosome">sex chromosome</a>. In plants that are sexually dimorphic, such as <i>Ginkgo biloba</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Judd_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Judd-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 203">: 203 </span></sup> the liverwort <i><a href="/wiki/Marchantia_polymorpha" title="Marchantia polymorpha">Marchantia polymorpha</a></i> or the dioecious species in the flowering plant genus <i><a href="/wiki/Silene" title="Silene">Silene</a></i>, sex may also be determined by sex chromosomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanurdzic_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanurdzic-42">[42]</a></sup> Non-genetic systems may use environmental cues, such as the <a href="/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination">temperature</a> during early development in <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, to determine the sex of the offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sex-determination_system" title="Sex-determination system">Sex determination</a> is often distinct from <a href="/wiki/Sexual_differentiation" title="Sexual differentiation">sex differentiation</a>. Sex determination is the designation for the development stage towards either male or female while sex differentiation is the pathway towards the development of the <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic">Genetic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg/220px-Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg/330px-Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg/440px-Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_XY_sex-determination.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Like humans and most other mammals, the common fruit fly has an <a href="/wiki/XY_sex-determination_system" title="XY sex-determination system">XY sex-determination system</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="XY_sex_determination">XY sex determination</span></h4>
<p>Humans and most other <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> have an <a href="/wiki/XY_sex-determination_system" title="XY sex-determination system">XY sex-determination system</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a> carries factors responsible for triggering male development, making XY sex determination mostly based on the presence or absence of the <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a>. It is the male <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a> that determines the sex of the offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18581056_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18581056-45">[45]</a></sup> In this system XX mammals typically are female and XY typically are male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup> However, individuals with <a href="/wiki/Klinefelter_syndrome" title="Klinefelter syndrome">XXY</a> or <a href="/wiki/XYY_syndrome" title="XYY syndrome">XYY</a> are males, while individuals with <a href="/wiki/Turner_syndrome" title="Turner syndrome">X</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_X_syndrome" title="Triple X syndrome">XXX</a> are females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup> Unusually, the <a href="/wiki/Platypus" title="Platypus">platypus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Monotreme" title="Monotreme">monotreme</a> mammal, has ten sex chromosomes; females have ten X chromosomes, and males have five X chromosomes and five Y chromosomes. Platypus egg cells all have five X chromosomes, whereas sperm cells can either have five X chromosomes or five Y chromosomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>XY sex determination is found in other organisms, including insects like the <a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">common fruit fly</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21047257_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21047257-47">[47]</a></sup> and some plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dellaporta_1993_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-dellaporta_1993-48">[48]</a></sup> In some cases, it is the number of X chromosomes that determines sex rather than the presence of a Y chromosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup> In the fruit fly individuals with XY are male and individuals with XX are female; however, individuals with XXY or XXX can also be female, and individuals with X can be males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="ZW_sex_determination">ZW sex determination</span></h4>
<p>In birds, which have a <a href="/wiki/ZW_sex-determination_system" title="ZW sex-determination system">ZW sex-determination system</a>, the W chromosome carries factors responsible for female development, and default development is male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In this case, ZZ individuals are male and ZW are female. It is the female <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a> that determines the sex of the offspring. This system is used by birds, some fish, and some <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">butterflies and moths</a> also have a ZW sex-determination system. Females can have Z, ZZW, and even ZZWW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="XO_sex_determination">XO sex determination</span></h4>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X0_sex-determination_system" title="X0 sex-determination system">X0 sex-determination system</a>, males have one X chromosome (X0) while females have two (XX). All other chromosomes in these diploid organisms are paired, but organisms may inherit one or two X chromosomes. This system is found in most <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, insects such as <a href="/wiki/Silverfish" title="Silverfish">silverfish</a> (<a href="/wiki/Apterygota" title="Apterygota">Apterygota</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoptera" title="Paleoptera">Paleoptera</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Exopterygota" title="Exopterygota">Exopterygota</a>), and some nematodes, crustaceans, and gastropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evolution_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evolution-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Gryllus" title="Gryllus">field crickets</a>, for example, insects with a single X chromosome develop as male, while those with two develop as female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the nematode <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i>, most worms are self-fertilizing hermaphrodites with an XX karyotype, but occasional abnormalities in chromosome inheritance can give rise to individuals with only one X chromosome—these X0 individuals are fertile males (and half their offspring are male).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="ZO_sex_determination">ZO sex determination</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-One_source plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-one_source" <p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Z0_sex-determination_system" title="Z0 sex-determination system">Z0 sex-determination system</a>, males have two Z chromosomes whereas females have one. This system is found in several species of moths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">Environmental</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_sex_determination" title="Environmental sex determination">Environmental sex determination</a></div>
<p>For many species, sex is not determined by inherited traits,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but instead by environmental factors such as temperature experienced during development or later in life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">fern</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratopteris" title="Ceratopteris">Ceratopteris</a></i> and other <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#homosporous" title="Glossary of botanical terms">homosporous</a> fern species, the default sex is hermaphrodite, but individuals which grow in soil that has previously supported hermaphrodites are influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromone</a> <a href="/wiki/Antheridiogen" title="Antheridiogen">antheridiogen</a> to develop as male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bonelliidae" title="Bonelliidae">bonelliidae larvae</a> can only develop as males when they encounter a female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sequential_hermaphroditism">Sequential hermaphroditism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG/220px-Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG/330px-Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG/440px-Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ocellaris_clownfish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clownfish" title="Clownfish">Clownfishes</a> are initially male; the largest fish in a group becomes female</div></div></div>
<p>Some species can change sex over the course of their lifespan, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Sequential_hermaphroditism" title="Sequential hermaphroditism">sequential hermaphroditism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-58">[58]</a></sup> Teleost fishes are the only vertebrate <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineage</a> where sequential hermaphroditism occurs. In <a href="/wiki/Clownfish" title="Clownfish">clownfish</a>, smaller fish are male, and the dominant and largest fish in a group becomes female; when a dominant female is absent, then her partner changes sex.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="changes sex to what?? (March 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In many <a href="/wiki/Wrasse" title="Wrasse">wrasses</a> the opposite is true—the fish are initially female and become male when they reach a certain size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Sequential hermaphroditism also occurs in plants such as <i><a href="/wiki/Arisaema_triphyllum" title="Arisaema triphyllum">Arisaema triphyllum</a></i>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature-dependent_sex_determination">Temperature-dependent sex determination</span></h4>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, including all <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> and most turtles, have temperature-dependent sex determination. In these species, the temperature experienced by the embryos during their development determines their sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-:4-25">[25]</a></sup> In some <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, for example, males are produced at lower temperatures than females; but <i><a href="/wiki/Alligator_snapping_turtle" title="Alligator snapping turtle">Macroclemys</a></i> females are produced at temperatures lower than 22 °C or above 28 °C, while males are produced in between those temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Haplodiploidy">Haplodiploidy</span></h4>
<p>Other insects,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Other than?? - no other examples given (March 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> including <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, use a <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploidy" title="Haplodiploidy">haplodiploid sex-determination system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Diploid bees and ants are generally female, and haploid individuals (which develop from unfertilized eggs) are male. This sex-determination system results in highly biased <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">sex ratios</a>, as the sex of offspring is determined by fertilization (<a href="/wiki/Arrhenotoky" title="Arrhenotoky">arrhenotoky</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-arrhenotoky" title="Pseudo-arrhenotoky">pseudo-arrhenotoky</a> resulting in males) rather than the assortment of chromosomes during meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_ratio">Sex ratio</span></h2>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">Sex ratio</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sex_ratio&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">sex ratio</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> of <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">females</a> to <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">males</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a>. The ratio tends to be 1:1 in most sexually reproducing species, which is explained by <a href="/wiki/Fisher%27s_principle" title="Fisher's principle">Fisher's principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_ratio_Hamilton67_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_ratio_Hamilton67-63">[63]</a></sup> Many species deviate from an even sex ratio, either periodically or permanently. Examples include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthenogenic" title="Parthenogenic">parthenogenic</a> species, periodically mating organisms such as aphids, some <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial</a> <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, bees, ants, and termites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
The human sex ratio is of particular interest to anthropologists and demographers. In human societies, sex ratios at birth may be considerably skewed by factors such as the age of mother at birth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_ratio_cdc_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_ratio_cdc-65">[65]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion" title="Sex-selective abortion">sex-selective abortion</a> and infanticide. Exposure to pesticides and other environmental contaminants may be a significant contributing factor as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> As of 2014, the global sex ratio at birth is estimated at 107 boys to 100 girls (1,000 boys per 934 girls).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>.</div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_differences">Sex differences</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_medicine" title="Sex differences in medicine">Sex differences in medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_intelligence" title="Sex differences in intelligence">Sex differences in intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_sex_differences" title="Neuroscience of sex differences">Neuroscience of sex differences</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_human_physiology" title="Sex differences in human physiology">Sex differences in human physiology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anisogamy" title="Anisogamy">Anisogamy</a> is the fundamental difference between male and female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:72_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:72-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> has stated that it is possible to interpret all the differences between the sexes as stemming from this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_humans" title="Sex differences in humans">Sex differences in humans</a> include a generally larger size and more body hair in men, while women have larger breasts, wider hips, and a higher body fat percentage. In other species, there may be differences in coloration or other features, and may be so pronounced that the different sexes may be mistaken for two entirely different taxa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CogBeh_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-CogBeh-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg/220px-Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg/330px-Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg/440px-Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Male_and_female_pheasant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Common_pheasant" title="Common pheasant">Common pheasants</a> are <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a> in both size and appearance.</div></div></div>
<p>In many animals and some plants, individuals of male and female sex differ in size and appearance, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-72">[72]</a></sup> Sexual dimorphism in animals is often associated with <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>—the mating competition between individuals of one sex vis-à-vis the opposite sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CogBeh_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-CogBeh-71">[71]</a></sup> In many cases, the male of a species is larger than the female. Mammal species with extreme sexual size dimorphism tend to have highly <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygynous</a> mating systems—presumably due to selection for success in <a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_competition" title="Intraspecific competition">competition</a> with other males—such as the <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">elephant seals</a>. Other examples demonstrate that it is the preference of females that drives sexual dimorphism, such as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Stalk-eyed_fly" title="Stalk-eyed fly">stalk-eyed fly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Females are the larger sex in a majority of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-72">[72]</a></sup> For instance, female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_black_widow" title="Southern black widow">southern black widow</a> spiders are typically twice as long as the males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> This size disparity may be associated with the cost of producing egg cells, which requires more nutrition than producing sperm: larger females are able to produce more eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexual dimorphism can be extreme, with males, such as some <a href="/wiki/Anglerfish" title="Anglerfish">anglerfish</a>, living <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasitically</a> on the female. Some plant species also exhibit dimorphism in which the females are significantly larger than the males, such as in the moss genus <i><a href="/wiki/Dicranum" title="Dicranum">Dicranum</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and the liverwort genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sphaerocarpos" title="Sphaerocarpos">Sphaerocarpos</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schuster_1984_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schuster_1984-77">[77]</a></sup> There is some evidence that, in these genera, the dimorphism may be tied to a sex chromosome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schuster_1984_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schuster_1984-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crum_&amp;_Anderson_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crum_&amp;_Anderson-78">[78]</a></sup> or to chemical signalling from females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In birds, males often have a more <a href="/wiki/Animal_coloration" title="Animal coloration">colourful</a> appearance and may have features (like the long tail of male peacocks) that would seem to put them at a disadvantage (e.g. bright colors would seem to make a bird more visible to predators). One proposed explanation for this is the <a href="/wiki/Handicap_principle" title="Handicap principle">handicap principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This hypothesis argues that, by demonstrating he can survive with such handicaps, the male is advertising his <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">genetic fitness</a> to females—traits that will benefit daughters as well, who will not be encumbered with such handicaps.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_characteristics">Sexual characteristics</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Sexual_characteristics" title="Sexual characteristics">Sexual characteristics</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sexual_characteristics&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Sexual_characteristics" title="Sexual characteristics">Sexual characteristics</a> are physical traits of an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> (typically of a sexually dimorphic organism) which are indicative of its biological sex. These can include <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ" title="Sex organ">sex organs</a> used for reproduction and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">secondary sex characteristics</a> which distinguish the sexes of a species, but which are not directly part of the reproductive system.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_differences_in_behavior">Sex differences in behavior</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_psychology" title="Sex differences in psychology">Sex differences in psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_sexual_behaviour" title="Animal sexual behaviour">Animal sexual behaviour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-reproductive_sexual_behavior_in_animals" title="Non-reproductive sexual behavior in animals">Non-reproductive sexual behavior in animals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_cognition" title="Sex differences in cognition">Sex differences in cognition</a></div>
<p>The sexes across gonochoric species usually differ in behavior. In most animal species females invest more in parental care,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> although in some species, such as some <a href="/wiki/Coucal" title="Coucal">coucals</a>, the males invest more <a href="/wiki/Parental_care" title="Parental care">parental care</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Females also tend to be more choosy for who they mate with,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> such as most bird species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Males tend to be more competitive for mating than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:11-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Purves-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Purves_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Purves_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPurvesSadavaOriansHeller2000">Purves WK, Sadava DE, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Orians" title="Gordon Orians">Orians GH</a>, Heller HC (2000). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kS-h84pMJw4C&amp;pg=PA736" rel="nofollow"><i>Life: The Science of Biology</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Publishers" title="Macmillan Publishers">Macmillan</a>. p. 736. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-3873-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-3873-2"><bdi>978-0-7167-3873-2</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190626184052/https://books.google.com/books?id=kS-h84pMJw4C&amp;pg=PA736" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 26 June 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 March</span> 2018</span>. <q>A single body can function as both male and female. Sexual reproduction requires both male and female haploid gametes. In most species, these gametes are produced by individuals that are either male or female. Species that have male and female members are called dioecious (from the Greek for 'two houses'). In some species, a single individual may possess both female and male reproductive systems. Such species are called monoecious ("one house") or hermaphroditic.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Life%3A+The+Science+of+Biology&amp;rft.pages=736&amp;rft.pub=Macmillan&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7167-3873-2&amp;rft.aulast=Purves&amp;rft.aufirst=WK&amp;rft.au=Sadava%2C+DE&amp;rft.au=Orians%2C+GH&amp;rft.au=Heller%2C+HC&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DkS-h84pMJw4C%26pg%3DPA736&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASex"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:9-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:9_3-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFRoyleSmisethKölliker2012">Royle NJ, Smiseth PT, Kölliker M (9 August 2012).  <a href="/wiki/Hanna_Kokko" title="Hanna Kokko">Kokko H</a>, Jennions M (eds.). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=K-EUDAAAQBAJ&amp;q=Sex+differences+in+parental+care&amp;pg=PR5" rel="nofollow"><i>The Evolution of Parental Care</i></a>. Oxford University Press. p. 103. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-969257-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-969257-6"><bdi>978-0-19-969257-6</bdi></a>. <q>The answer is that there is an agreement by convention: individuals producing the smaller of the two gamete types-sperm or pollen- are males, and those producing larger gametes-eggs or ovules- are females.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Evolution+of+Parental+Care&amp;rft.pages=103&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2012-08-09&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-969257-6&amp;rft.aulast=Royle&amp;rft.aufirst=NJ&amp;rft.au=Smiseth%2C+PT&amp;rft.au=K%C3%B6lliker%2C+M&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DK-EUDAAAQBAJ%26q%3DSex%2Bdifferences%2Bin%2Bparental%2Bcare%26pg%3DPR5&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASex"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-:72-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:72_68-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFWhitfield2004">Whitfield J (June 2004). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC423151" rel="nofollow">"Everything you always wanted to know about sexes"</a>. <i>PLOS Biology</i>. <b>2</b> (6): e183. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pbio.0020183" rel="nofollow">10.1371/journal.pbio.0020183</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC423151" rel="nofollow">423151</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15208728" rel="nofollow">15208728</a>. <q>One thing biologists do agree on is that males and females count as different sexes. And they also agree that the main difference between the two is gamete size: males make lots of small gametes—sperm in animals, pollen in plants—and females produce a few big eggs.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=PLOS+Biology&amp;rft.atitle=Everything+you+always+wanted+to+know+about+sexes&amp;rft.volume=2&amp;rft.issue=6&amp;rft.pages=e183&amp;rft.date=2004-06&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC423151%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F15208728&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1371%2Fjournal.pbio.0020183&amp;rft.aulast=Whitfield&amp;rft.aufirst=J&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC423151&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASex"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPierce2012">Pierce BA (2012). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=z4pXRaZAkdkC&amp;q=Gamete+size" rel="nofollow"><i>Genetics: A Conceptual Approach</i></a>. W. H. Freeman. p. 74. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4292-3252-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4292-3252-4"><bdi>978-1-4292-3252-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Genetics%3A+A+Conceptual+Approach&amp;rft.pages=74&amp;rft.pub=W.+H.+Freeman&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4292-3252-4&amp;rft.aulast=Pierce&amp;rft.aufirst=BA&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dz4pXRaZAkdkC%26q%3DGamete%2Bsize&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASex"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDawkins2016"><a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Dawkins, Richard</a> (2016). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ekonDAAAQBAJ&amp;q=the+selfish+gene+battle+of+the+sexes+2016" rel="nofollow"><i>The Selfish Gene</i></a>. Oxford University Press. pp. 183–184. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-878860-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-878860-7"><bdi>978-0-19-878860-7</bdi></a>. <q>However, there is one fundamental feature of the sexes which can be used to label males as males, and females as females, throughout animals and plants. This is that the sex cells or 'gametes' of males are much smaller and more numerous than the gametes of females. This is true whether we are dealing with animals or plants. One group of individuals has large sex cells, and it is convenient to use the word female for them. The other group, which it is convenient to call male, has small sex cells. The difference is especially pronounced in reptiles and in birds, where a single egg cell is big enough and nutritious enough to feed a developing baby for. Even in humans, where the egg is microscopic, it is still many times larger than the sperm. As we shall see, it is possible to interpret all the other differences between the sexes as stemming from this one basic difference.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Selfish+Gene&amp;rft.pages=183-184&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-878860-7&amp;rft.aulast=Dawkins&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DekonDAAAQBAJ%26q%3Dthe%2Bselfish%2Bgene%2Bbattle%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bsexes%2B2016&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASex"></span></span>
</li>
